    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1531173038" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1531173038" />
    <a title="Daiane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32806732-daiane"><img alt="Daiane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1457143503p2/32806732.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1531173038?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daiane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daiane" href="/user/show/32806732-daiane">Daiane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32806732-daiane?shelf=surprisingly-good">surprisingly-good</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32806732-daiane?shelf=badass-chick">badass-chick</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32806732-daiane?shelf=blood">blood</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32806732-daiane?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32806732-daiane?shelf=we-are-all-mad-here">we-are-all-mad-here</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32806732-daiane?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32806732-daiane?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32806732-daiane?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32806732-daiane?shelf=revenge">revenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1531173038" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2676314740876373435"><i>
  <b>“I’ll walk forever with stories inside me that the people I love the most can never hear.”</b>
</i><br/><br/>O.M.G.<br/>I mean<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454180630ra/17928530.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>THIS BOOK IS AWESOME!<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1433627447ra/15122909.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I can't even start putting into words my feelings at the moment I finished this book. <br/>This woman was able to mess with my mind. It made me surprised, confused, angry, sad, amused... I DON'T KNOW!!!<br/><br/>Basically this girl cannot have a single day in peace. Even when she tries to tell the true that is taking her sleep away, nobody believes her. Not even I believed her. I have </span>
  <span id="freeText2676314740876373435" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>“I’ll walk forever with stories inside me that the people I love the most can never hear.”</b>
</i><br><br>O.M.G.<br>I mean<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454180630ra/17928530.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>THIS BOOK IS AWESOME!<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1433627447ra/15122909.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I can't even start putting into words my feelings at the moment I finished this book. <br>This woman was able to mess with my mind. It made me surprised, confused, angry, sad, amused... I DON'T KNOW!!!<br><br>Basically this girl cannot have a single day in peace. Even when she tries to tell the true that is taking her sleep away, nobody believes her. Not even I believed her. I have to confess that if in the end she was just completely insane and everything was a lie, I wouldn't be so surprised. But right now, I can believe how the story developed. I mean, I have a lot of complains...<br><br>The first one is about her family. With everything that is happening to her, and to them, it's hard to have someone on her side.<br><br><i>
  <b>“But when everyone tells you you're crazy and no one believes you when you swear you aren't, a small part of you will always wonder if they're right.” </b>
</i><br><br>And her mother... I know she is just worried and trying to help, but I just couldn't stand her. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454180630ra/17928531.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Still, I'm really hoping that Daniel, her brother, would just discover everything and try to help her. Or at least give her the benefit of the doubt. He is one of my favorite characters over the story and their confidence in each other is so touching!<br><br><i>
  <b>“I wish she knew that I think she’s the most hilarious person on Earth. And that whenever she’s not home, I feel like I’m missing my partner in crime. I wish she knew that she’s really Mom’s favorite, the princess she always wanted. That Mom used to dress her up like a little doll and parade her around like Mara was her greatest achievement. I wish Mara knew that I never minded, because she’s my favorite too.”</b>
</i><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454180630ra/17928532.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1436403813ra/15453437.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>All this nobody-believes-me situation makes Mara lie to the world.<br><br><i>
  <b>“I pulled out my sketchbook to work on an asinine project I’d been assigned: pick an emotion and draw it. I wanted to draw a raised middle finger, but I would draw a kitten instead. Normal people love kittens.”</b>
</i><br><br>Creating a mask separating what she meant and what she had to mean. What she was and what others expected her to be. And how free every one of us want to be and are judged or labeled as different.<br><br><i>
  <b>“Everyone is a little crazy. The only difference between us and them is that they hide it better.” </b>
</i><br><br>The only person she can count with, and I'm really glad, is Noah. His character development was really thought. I still have my problems with him even though a lot was explained. I hate how sometimes I can't feel him and others he is all full of cheesiness. <br><br><i>
  <b>"I would rather die with the taste of you on my tongue than live and never touch you again. I’m in love with you, Mara. I love you. No matter what you do.”<br>“We’re only seventeen,”<br>“Fuck seventeen. If I were to live a thousand years, I would belong to you for all of them. If we were to live a thousand lives, I would want to make you mine in each one.”</b>
</i><br><br>And I still hate how he makes Mara feels so insecure and all Mary Sue. But still, he is really helpful to the story and I couldn't resist to his charm. Specially when we get to see more about his true self.<br><br><i>
  <b>"He still looked like an arrogant prince. But only now could I see that his crown was broken."</b>
</i><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454180630ra/17928533.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Jamie is also back. However, differently from others YAs, I loved how here there's only the friendship and respect. There are no forced feelings and Jamie has his own demons to fight. He is not there for the purpose of destroying the leads' love story. He is truly alive.<br><br><i>
  <b>“We are far too screwed up for a goddamned love triangle.”</b>
</i><br><br>And even though it's starting to be a cliche to have THE different character to get the readers to be amused about how authors are giving attention to others than blue-eyes-blondes, I still loved Jamie. <br><br><i>
  <b>“Words have power. And I may be privileged and have a higher IQ than any of our former teachers, but when people look at me? They see a black, male teenager. And there is nothing quite as frightening to some folks as an angry young black man.”</b>
</i><br><br>We are also introduced to many other incredible characters that give us many secrets. Some of them are good. Some are bad. <br><br><i>
  <b>“Sometimes, the biggest secrets you can only tell a stranger.”</b>
</i><br><br>Still, the characters were well written and some were nicely complex. Strangely enough, even though they were insane, I kinda liked them.<br><br><i>
  <b>“It’s for the voodoo doll, It looks just like you.”<br>You can’t respond to a statement like that. There’s just nothing to say.</b>
</i><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1436726236ra/15496398.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Like before, I must praise the writing style. It's witty and has a small amount of irony and comedy. It has beautiful and meaningful quotes that made me enjoy my reading even more. Again Hodkin made me doubt my mind through the unreliable narration. I really enjoyed all the mystery and twists. With all the tension and madness, this book was able to blow my mind. I seriously think that even though people talk a lot about the final book being horrible, I would still recommend this series for those who are in for unexpected turns and mixed feelings. And be prepared for lies.<br><br><i>
  <b>"You will love him to ruins.”<br>All the pain I had ever felt was just practice for this moment.</b>
</i><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1433649611ra/15126813.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="2676314740876373435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_330464344" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/330464344" />
    <a title="B" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5206717-b"><img alt="B" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/330464344?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B" href="/user/show/5206717-b">B</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781442421790" href="/book/show/12950372-the-evolution-of-mara-dyer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206717-b?shelf=i-want-it-right-now">i-want-it-right-now</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206717-b?shelf=judge-a-book-by-the-cover">judge-a-book-by-the-cover</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206717-b?shelf=highly-anticipating-2012">highly-anticipating-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206717-b?shelf=almost-fell-asleep">almost-fell-asleep</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206717-b?shelf=hooray-for-masochism">hooray-for-masochism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206717-b?shelf=i-totally-saw-that-coming">i-totally-saw-that-coming</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206717-b?shelf=disappointments">disappointments</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206717-b?shelf=2012-reads">2012-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206717-b?shelf=preorder">preorder</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer330464344" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10067361850217974879">Have you ever seen the movie <i>The Strangers</i>? If so, do you remember that one scene where the woman was in the foreground of her house, panicking, and in the background you see one of the white-masked strangers inside her house, watching her? Such a simple and subtle scene, that was. There was no music, no big bang to draw your attention to the stranger - nothing. Just complete and utter silence, aside from the sound of the woman taking short, panicked breaths. The subtlety of that one particular </span>
  <span id="freeText10067361850217974879" style="display:none">Have you ever seen the movie <i>The Strangers</i>? If so, do you remember that one scene where the woman was in the foreground of her house, panicking, and in the background you see one of the white-masked strangers inside her house, watching her? Such a simple and subtle scene, that was. There was no music, no big bang to draw your attention to the stranger - nothing. Just complete and utter silence, aside from the sound of the woman taking short, panicked breaths. The subtlety of that one particular scene, and the fact that if I weren't expecting a big bang as per usual in horror movies and looking in the background for the big shocker, I would have probably missed the stranger standing there, watching her, made that scene more terrifying to me than it would have been if there had been the big bang and the loud music drawing my attention to the background. <br /><br />My whole point for describing that one particular scene in this review was to make a point that for me, subtlety in horror is more scary and effective than anything else. At times, when watching horror movies, in your face scenes with incredibly loud music <i>do</i> entertain me, and, at times, scare me (most notably in the movie <i>Insidious</i>), but usually, for a scene to linger with me and make me lose sleep, the scene has to be subtle - so subtle that I could miss it. Just like that scene in <i>The Strangers</i>. <br /><br />And that is where I feel <i>The Evolution of Mara Dyer</i> took one large misstep concerning its creepy elements. There are, of course, other faults in the novel, such as the fact that I found it to be way too long drawn out in the second half and that it ends in yet another cop-out of a cliffhanger to stretch out the series when everything could have easily been resolved in this book, but I think the 'in-your-face' creepy scenes may have been the most prominent and effective detraction to my enjoyment in <i>The Evolution of Mara Dyer</i>. In my pre-review, I wrote that the creepy scenes in this book were like someone was banging pots and pans while screaming at me to look at something - with no subtlety whatsoever. (I'll attach my pre-review below this review, for those who are curious to read it.) <br /><br />Clearly Hodkin's intention with these scenes were to freak readers out and to have them lose sleep, and it probably will freak many readers out as some of the scenes are genuinely creepy. There were times when reading this that I was a bit freaked out, but the scene(s) that freaked me out won't <i>linger</i>, and that's really what I'm looking for in horror - for scenes to linger with me. Of course, this is a personal issue for me with this book, and if subtlety - or lack thereof - in scenes intended to be creepy don't make a difference to you, then this will not be a problem for you. However, if you are the type of reader and movie-watcher that I am, where subtlety actually <i>matters</i> then I'd look elsewhere for your ideal Halloween read. <br /><br />That isn't to say you should skip reading this entirely, though, because I'd definitely recommend it to quite a few people. If you, like me, were in love with the plot of <i>The Unbecoming of Mara Dyer</i> but were less than enthused by romance completely overthrowing the plot, you will be relieved to know that <i>The Evolution of Mara Dyer</i> is much more plot-focused and less romantically involved than its predecessor. While I did think that there may have been <i>too</i> much plot, to the point where things got completely out of control and crazy, I found it to be entertaining nonetheless. Unfortunately, there are also a few negative similarities between <i>Evolution</i> and <i>Unbecoming</i>, such as the fact that both are way too long drawn out to the point of frustration, having the reader (ie: me) wondering when the book will come to an end, and more importantly <i>hoping</i> it will finally come to an end. <br /><br />And while I did thoroughly enjoy - maybe even love - the first half of <i>The Evolution of Mara Dyer</i>, I found that it lost its way in the second half, leading up to a disappointing finale ending with, you guessed it, another huge cliffhanger. But this time, unlike the first, I don't have much hope for the <i>Mara Dyer series</i> anymore. The cliffhanger at the end of <i>The Unbecoming of Mara Dyer</i> left me with a feeling hope that the series would eventually become awesome over time, and more importantly it made me care for what would happen next. But the difference this time is that I don't care, and I think that is the biggest thing I can say about my enjoyment in this series thus far: that I just don't care anymore. <br /><br /><br /><b>Pre-Review</b><br /><br /><br />I probably won't be reviewing this, because I've already tried and it frustrated the hell out of me, but what you need to know was that this book, like its review which remains unwritten, frustrated the living hell out of me. I enjoyed the ride throughout the first half, finding it to be creepy and mysterious, but as I hit the fifty percent mark I found myself soon bored by the cliche happenings that were just trying too hard. For things to be creepy to me, they must be subtle, and not "WHOA - LOOK OVER THERE! SOMETHING CREEPY'S HAPPENING! NO, SERIOUSLY, LOOK OVER THERE. YOU'RE GOING TO MISS THIS. GOD DAMMIT LOOK OVER THERE SOMETHING'S HAPPENING! *bangs pots and pans* LOOOOOOOK!". And that, my friends, is what <i>The Evolution of Mara Dyer</i>'s creepy elements felt like to me. They were trying too hard to get my attention and to freak me out, and I think it goes without saying that it did not. Some of the happenings were interesting, but there were just so many of them that each time something new happened I found myself rolling my eyes. And, similarly to its predecessor, <i>The Evolution of Mara Dyer</i> overstayed its welcome, which is probably the main reason I was so frustrated by it. The first half was fun to read, but the second half, to me, was so boring and repetitive and never-ending that I found myself skimming pages. And all of the "creepy" happenings led up to an incredibly anticlimactic finale that, once again, ends in a pretty sizable cliffhanger. <br /><br />But the thing is, I don't really care anymore. The cliffhanger at the end of <i>The Unbecoming of Mara Dyer</i> had me caring about what would happen next, even if I didn't like the entire novel. The ending of <i>The Unbecoming of Mara Dyer</i> made me have <i>hope</i> that the series would get better and more plot-related. And while I can say that <i>The Evolution of Mara Dyer</i> is an overall stronger companion to <i>Unbecoming</i>, I just can't bring myself to care anymore. I know a lot of my friends will really enjoy this, and some already <i>have</i>, but it frustrated me with its repetitiveness, its overly long drawn out stay, and its shoddy attempt at adding another installment with a cop-out cliffhanger when everything could have been resolved in this book, or even the first. But the difference is this time I won't be suckered into reading the next installment. <br /><br />First half: 4.5 stars<br />Second half: 2 stars<br />Final verdict: A very unsure 3 stars (don't go by my rating, go by my review, it's more accurate)<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[And god I hope Noah's really dead. Of course he's not but man that'd be just great. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="10067361850217974879" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5705623-kristalia?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer649583024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6610139262501644910"><b>Final rating: 3.5/5 stars</b><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>This was the boy I loved. A little bit messy. A little bit ruined. A beautiful disaster.<br/>Just like me. </i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br/><br/><b> The last 20% were so worthy of 3.5 STARS. </b><br/><br/>I don't know if it is a standard for Michelle Hodkin or if i always get bored until i reach 70% of the book? Because it is the deal with me and Mara Dyer series. But, this time, i got some answers. But! I got confused as hell! And i loved that! Can you imagine? I actually loved this book. More than the first one anyw</span>
  <span id="freeText6610139262501644910" style="display:none"><b>Final rating: 3.5/5 stars</b><br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>This was the boy I loved. A little bit messy. A little bit ruined. A beautiful disaster.<br>Just like me. </i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br><br><b> The last 20% were so worthy of 3.5 STARS. </b><br><br>I don't know if it is a standard for Michelle Hodkin or if i always get bored until i reach 70% of the book? Because it is the deal with me and Mara Dyer series. But, this time, i got some answers. But! I got confused as hell! And i loved that! Can you imagine? I actually loved this book. More than the first one anyway.<br><br>Now that i had idea what was going on, i started to suspect a lot of things and to have my theories, but i don't think i have guessed them. i was close but i didn't see that coming in the end. That ending... everything there is suspicious, because Mara didn't witness it so it doesn't mean it <i> actually</i> happened, so i don't believe it. It was a massive cliffhanger, but surprisingly it didn't affect me in any way, because i could have seen it coming. <br><br>Once again, the problem i had with this book is the longevity of it. It is always unnecessary long, some may not agree, but there were many useless parts which i didn't find amusing or dramatic or anything. They were just boring.<br><br>● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ●<br><br>But the story this time surprised me, with some plot twists and turn arounds, and i was quite immersed into it. <br><br>I won't reveal much about the story, since i don't know how i would write the synopsis but it was creepier, weirder and definitely better than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8591107.The_Unbecoming_of_Mara_Dyer__Mara_Dyer___1_" title="The Unbecoming of Mara Dyer (Mara Dyer, #1) by Michelle Hodkin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Unbecoming of Mara Dyer</a>. But those India flashbacks were quite annoying because i don't understand their point. Or i just couldn't care less.<br><br>But the worst thing: <b>
  <i> i need the answers because now i feel like i have been given more questions than answers! And it's making me lose my mind! </i>
</b><br><br><br>____________________________________________<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>CHARACTERS</u>
  </i>
</b>: <br>____________________________________________<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>"You can’t hurt me the way you think you can. But even if you could? I would rather die with the taste of you on my tongue than live and never touch you again. I’m in love with you, Mara. I love you. No matter what you do.”<br>My breath caught in my throat. No matter what. The words were a promise, a promise I didn’t know if anyone could keep.<br>“We’re only seventeen,” I said quietly.<br>“Fuck seventeen.” His eyes and voice were defiant. “If I were to live a thousand years, I would belong to you for all of them. If we were to live a thousand lives, I would want to make you mine in each one.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br><br><br><br>♦ <b>
  <i>
    <u>Mara </u>
  </i>
</b><br><br>The best way to describe Mara in this book would be obsession: obsessed about Noah, obsessed about her feelings, obsessed with her fears, obsessed with everything that has ever happened to her. But, she was kind of better in this book than in the last one and i kind of liked her too. But she was still annoying sometimes, sadly. Other than that, she is not a bad character at all.<br><br>● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ●<br><br>♦ <b>
  <i>
    <u>Noah</u>
  </i>
</b><br><br>The thing about Noah is that he is way to secretive. He wants to help, but he barely says anything about himself and his situation. And all the money stuff is getting on my nerves :( Somehow in some scenes he didn't felt <i>believable</i> enough. But it didn't stop him from being still the same cocky arrogant idiot. <br><br>● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ●<br><br>♦ <b>
  <i>
    <u>Everyone else</u>
  </i>
</b>:<br><br>I am split opinion about <b>Mara's parents.</b> They are supportive of her, but what irritated me is that they never listen to her. They never give her the chance to say the whole story. But in the normal situation, what they did, would probably every parent do. <br><br><b>Noah's parents </b>on the other hand were ... absent... they didn't have any point except to tell us how Noah feels because of them. <br><br>I loved <b>Daniel</b>, he is just so freaking adorable and awesome, and i must say that i loved<b> Jamie</b> too, though he should give chances sometimes instead of reacting.<br><br><b>Other female characters </b>were either crazy or bat*hit insane, or just wrong. I don't think there was any normal girl aside from Mara...if she can be considered normal anyway.<br><br><b>Jude</b>... sadistic bastard would fit. And i have so many questions about him that i need answers. <br>____________________________________________<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>OVERALL</u>
  </i>
</b>: <br>____________________________________________<br><br>Not a bad sequel, i liked it and enjoyed it, but like i said before, i had problem with it's size. But this series has interesting story, it was just not so well executed but who knows what will third book give us? Hopefully more answers.<br>____________________________________________<br><br>● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ●<br>____________________________________________ <br><br><b> MY MARA DYER REVIEWS: </b><br><br> ◉ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/649583019" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Unbecoming of Mara Dyer (Mara Dyer, #1) </a><br> ◉ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/649583024" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Evolution of Mara Dyer (Mara Dyer, #2) </a><br><br><br><b>

<div id="review_2002353685" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_993659627" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/993659627" />
    <a title="Jess" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17389678-jess"><img alt="Jess" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420145890p2/17389678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/993659627?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jess" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jess" href="/user/show/17389678-jess">Jess</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17389678-jess?shelf=all-time-favourite-reads">all-time-favourite-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17389678-jess?shelf=swoon-worthy-covers">swoon-worthy-covers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer993659627" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6857647523176510277"><blockquote>
  <b>THE SCIENTIFIC THEORY: aka I’ve scoured the net, and yet none have mentioned the following theory pertaining to <i>The Retribution of Mara Dyer</i></b>
</blockquote> (edited to include <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/227463.A_Clockwork_Orange' title='A Clockwork Orange by Anthony Burgess' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Clockwork Orange</a>--found at the bottom.) (October: <b>edited to include the Daniel Myer theory</b>)<br/><br/><blockquote>
<b>This all links back to Biotechnology</b>. Otherwise known as <b>David Shaw</b>, Noah Shaw’s father. </blockquote><br/><br/>Noah’s father owns a megacorporation that all stems from research in biotechnology. According to the <i>UN Convention on Biological Diversity, Act 2</i>, biotec</span>
  <span id="freeText6857647523176510277" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>THE SCIENTIFIC THEORY: aka I’ve scoured the net, and yet none have mentioned the following theory pertaining to <i>The Retribution of Mara Dyer</i></b>
</blockquote> (edited to include <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/227463.A_Clockwork_Orange" title="A Clockwork Orange by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clockwork Orange</a>--found at the bottom.) (October: <b>edited to include the Daniel Myer theory</b>)<br><br><blockquote>
<b>This all links back to Biotechnology</b>. Otherwise known as <b>David Shaw</b>, Noah Shaw’s father. </blockquote><br><br>Noah’s father owns a megacorporation that all stems from research in biotechnology. According to the <i>UN Convention on Biological Diversity, Act 2</i>, biotechnology is:<br><br><blockquote>
  <i> “any technological application that uses biological systems, living organisms or derivatives thereof, to make or modify products or processes for specific use”</i>
</blockquote><br><br>In essence, Noah’s father dabbles in the field of genetic modification (or so I think that he does. I’ll be honest with you, I did absolutely no science subjects in my senior studies). <br><br>In <i>The Evolution of Mara Dyer</i>, Mara’s father says a passing comment about David Shaw’s company: <br><br><blockquote>
  <b> “There were some federal indictments handed down a year ago for the executives of one of his megacorporation’s subsidiaries—Aurora Biotech? Euphrates International […]” </b>
</blockquote><br><br>Noah’s father is by far the most enigmatic character mentioned in the passing. If he was just Daddy Warbucks, why have all these constant, and consistent insinuations and passing comments about his father’s involvement in biotechnology. We know from the above quote that something shady is cooking up in his <i>research</i>. <br><br>This oddity comes up once again when Noah mentions that his father needs security. Why? It would seem that the biotechnological work that his father doesn’t isn’t so simple. Once again, the company is insinuated to dabble deeper in something that is not quite…<i>moral</i>. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b> “A euphemism for ‘playing God,’ according to the religious and environmental groups that hate his subsidiaries.”</b>
</blockquote><br><br>Quite odd, that is. David Shaw is implied to be a person who enjoys manipulating natural forces, a man<b> “playing God” </b>. <br><br>Let’s diverge a bit and jump to the medical transcript at the end. What struck me as <b>out of place</b> were the words on both Lowe sibling transcripts:<b>
  <i> “artificially manifested, Lenaurd Protocol, early induction”</i>
</b>. Why artificial? When you think artificial, one would think <b>against nature</b>--as though someone defied the natural world, <i>played God</i> as they say. Not to mention the <b>Lenaurd Protocol</b>. The word protocol is commonly associated with experimentations and programs, one that could potentially military. <br><br>But the most significant part was<b> “early induction”</b>. Jude desperately wants Claire back—but why? Why not his family? Why not his old life? What is his endgame? When questioned by Mara, his answers always come full circle—back to Claire. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b> “ ‘What about your family?...<br>‘Claire was my family.’ Jude’s voice was different now. Less harsh.” </b>
</blockquote><br><br>This naturally got me thinking. Why would he give no care to the wellbeing of his mother and father, who mysteriously evaporated upon the deaths of both siblings? Why of course! Unless Claire was his only true <i>family</i>. <b>Early induction</b>--whatever experiment they were entangled in, they were in it <b>together</b>. Either both were orphans—related or not, I have no answer to—or were both joined together in the origins of this<b> “artificial manifestation” </b>. Could they perhaps be the first byproducts of a genetic mutation? I’m thinking that the two either originated and were artificially formed, or were experimented on, so as to encompass the G1821—genetic experimentation that defied nature and defied God. A couple of you have mentioned the link between the genetic code and Jude and Claire’s address: 1281 Live Oak Court. I think this cracks onto something. If Jude and Claire truly identified with this experiment—G1821—then perhaps this is reflected in their address. If they had no one else but each other then 1821, or it reversed, would, to them, be like <b>home</b>--a place of shelter, and a place of cultivation. <br><br>Then one would begin to think, Jude and Dr. Kells seem to be in it together. Dr. Kells mentions a blind study. But studies <b>must be financed</b>. This study went through a hell of an effort to conjoin all these counterparts together, one must stop to think:<b>who is funding it?</b>. And the only person that I can stop to think of now with that kind of cash is Daddy Warbucks, a.k.a the lovely David Shaw. <br><br>I’ve also come to convince myself further that biotechnology is involved. In the beginning of <i>The Unbecoming of Mara Dyer</i>, Mara implies that the following events are told following their occurrence. She states that she is in New York City. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b> “I’m afraid David’s in New York at the moment” </b>
</blockquote><br><br>That’s right. Guess whose papa heads an office in NY: Noah. The note forewarning the series has Mara mentioning her<b> “lawyer"</b>. Why have a lawyer, and why, pray tell, would she also be in NYC. Unless, we get a hopeful conclusion. Or unless she’s suing the hell out of a certain biotechnology giant for unlawful, unmoral, unethical genetic mutative experimentation.<br><br>But then you begin to wonder, what does David Shaw get out of this? What could possibly be his endgame? Now I couldn’t possibly tell you that, but I’ve sure as hell had a long think over it. Here is when <b>Granny Mara</b> becomes a central figure. <br><br><b>Granny Mara</b> went to Cambridge. Granny Mara went to Cambridge and studied at Darwin College. Darwin College was a tribute to Charles Darwin, the one and only Darwin who penned the literary genius and scientific work of art, <b>
  <i>On the Origin of Species</i>
</b>. That’s right, this was the very work that explored the idea of natural selection and genetic superiority, one that has aided in the segregation of the weak from the strong; an evolution of society. What are the odds? Genetics, once again. The Mara Dyer series deals with kids who have advanced abilities, ones that have left them superior to the rest of the human race. Darwin College is graduate-only, and it assumed to be the place where Granny Mara did her PHD. It is established that Granny Mara knew Noah’s mother, but it is also suggested that perhaps she could have known his father as well. But father remains an enigma:<br><br><blockquote>
  <b> “ ‘Can you ask your dad? Maybe he remembers this.’ …<br>‘He won’t talk about her.’ Noah’s voice went voice went flat. “ </b>
</blockquote><br><br>Granny Mara was at Cambridge for her PHD, and yet she never finished.<br><br><blockquote>
  <b> “She was in school for her PHD, but she never finished. I think that always bothered her.”</b>
</blockquote><br><br>If it bothered her, then<b> what could have been so detrimental that it stopped her from completing her doctorate?</b> Granny Mara also then becomes very, very paranoid, but what could have induced such a thing? <br><br><blockquote>
  <b> “…when she went inside to pay, a man asked me for directions…And just as he got back in his car and drove away, your grandmother ran out. She wanted to know everything—what he wanted, what he said—she was wild” </b>
</blockquote><br><br>Perhaps she was running from something, from <i>someone</i>.<b> A man?</b> What if, in her liaison with Noah’s mother, and possibly his father, she became entangled in a genetic experimentation? But entertain this thought: what if it went wrong, if she got cold feet and if she realised its ethical implications? Cold her paranoia be induced out of a fear of David Shaw? <br><br>Now if we labour under my theory here and assume that Dr. Kells is under advisement from David Shaw, then it would explain why she was at the asylum, recording the video of the building’s collapse. If Granny Mara became paranoid then she must have had something to hide. But this something could just as easily have been a <i>someone</i>. If she was involved in any form of genetic experimentation, especially in the field of study genetic superiority and genetic anomaly, and her genetics were central (esp when comparing Granny Mara, Noah's mum and his father, Granny would be the oldest of the group, perhaps making her genes more "original" then the others) then for anything else to continue they would need a descendent from Granny Mara. Someone who possessed the same genetic mutation. Her own daughter did not exhibit any special abilities, and she was very cautious of this, but what if she recognized Mara’s ability, and as a precaution, decided to cut all links of association between herself and Mara, to keep her <b>safe</b>. There is little to link Granny Mara to Mara in the first place. Granny Mara refused to have photographs taken and Mara’s last name is her fathers. So what if Jude and Claire, the mysterious brother and sister, were charged with infiltrating Rachel and Mara? They would not have been sure that Mara was the correct descendant of Granny Mara (a seemingly original carrier of G1821). I think the asylum collapse was a setup—a test of Mara’s ability. One she passed, but at a cost. It would validate Jude’s anger, because perhaps Claire was not meant to die. They underestimated Mara’s power, and perhaps Claire was a cost—one that now fuels Jude’s retribution. <br><br>You guys are probably thinking if David Shaw is so central to the plot then why have Noah in the way. But think about—Noah’s supposed death has eliminated him from the chessboard. He has been eliminated from this chase for Mara. When probed about what David Shaw thought of his son’s relationship with Mara, Noah replied: <br><br><blockquote>
  <b> “He thinks I’m a fool” </b>
</blockquote> <br><br>That can be taken with several meanings, but the one I like to think is he’s a fool, because now he has become central to the plot. David doesn’t want his son mixed in with this, and thus he is eliminated from the game.<br><br>Despite everything, I still think science is so integral to the overall endgame. Why else would it be mentioned so much. We, the audience, are so occupied by the supernatural shenanigans, the impossible and the love component that when science sneaks it’s way in, perhaps we just don’t regard it as much. It is so minute, yet it is the minute details that count. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b> “ ‘It’s science—‘<br>‘It’s madness,’ he said. </b>
</blockquote><br><br><blockquote>
  <b> “ ‘Why do you keep reaching for science?’<br>‘Why do you keep reaching for magic?’”</b>
</blockquote><br><br>These are just some thoughts to think about. I’ve rambled and this is the length of an essay now. An essay of incoherent thoughts. Brilliant going Jess.<br><br>Ps. This may be edited from time to time, purely when I think of new things or if I’ve realised that I’ve left a particular thing out. <br><br>PPS. There's more, kids. I'm just rolling up to 3am and pretty much too exhausted to piece together coherent sentences. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>The first of many forthcoming edits: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/227463.A_Clockwork_Orange" title="A Clockwork Orange by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clockwork Orange</a> edition</b>
</blockquote><br><br>In the preview of <i>The Retribution of Mara Dyer</i> cork board, a particular image caught my eye.<br><br>(Click here for said image: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-0PY4ZEG2oqs/UYlBhgQADGI/AAAAAAAAB9w/M7nl9jfe_vA/s1600/Retribution+corkboard.jpg">http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-0PY4ZEG2oqs...</a>)<br><br>That image right at the centre bottom, a <b>cog eye</b>. Now that struck me straight away. I don’t have a photographic memory and what a blessing it would be if I did have one. Instead I remember semblances of certain things, and in my mind I can link them back to certain places, or locations, of where I’ve seen them (tangent, I know, but essentially it’s like if you asked me for a definition of a concept I wouldn’t remember, but I would remember the concept you want to find and remember it’s location in a book, eg. on the right hand towards the top of a page in the first third of the book). I had one of those moments. Now of course I’ve seen that image. It’s the cover of the controversial <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/227463.A_Clockwork_Orange" title="A Clockwork Orange by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clockwork Orange</a>. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1405253580i/10372393.jpg" width="310" height="500" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>How odd, it would seem, to include the image of such a book, but then I did some research. In the introduction to the 1986 publication of the novella, Burgess wrote the following: <br><br><blockquote>
  <i> “By definition, a human being is endowed with free will. He can use this to choose between good and evil. If he can only perform good or only perform evil, then he is a clockwork orange—meaning he has the appearance of an organism lovely with colour and juice but is in fact only a clockwork toy to be wound up by God or the Devil or (since this is increasingly replacing both) the Almighty State. It is as inhumane to be totally good as it is to be to ally evil. The important thing is moral choice. Evil has to exist along with good, in order that moral choice may operate. Life is sustained by the grinding opposition of moral entities.” </i>
</blockquote> <br><br>Notice in particular the line <b>
  <i>“He has the appearance of an organism lovely with colour and juice but is in fact only a clockwork toy to be wound up by God” </i>
</b>. Jude’s appearance depicts a character who is seemingly “harmless”—an All-American boy. He is the orange. As we’ve seen him, Jude seems only capable of performing evil deeds. He has been a one dimensional character, only slightly vulnerable at the mention of his sister Claire. In that way, it would seem that Jude is a <b>clockwork orange</b>—at the mercy of being<i> “wound up by God” (and let’s reiterate, who likes <b>”Playing God”</b>? David Shaw). There are times where Jude’s speech is described as being <b>”mechanical”</b> almost as though he has no control over what he does. Seeing how impassioned Jude is, motivated by his sister, one would think that he would want simple retribution—Mara’s life for Claire’s. So why is he insistent that Mara has the capability to bring Claire back to life? Jamie’s medical transcript states him as<b><i> “manifesting, induced”</i></b>. Mara’s has a simple<b><i> “manifesting”</i></b></i>. It would seem that whilst both have shown signs of abilities, Jamie may have had his limits and ultimate capacities <i>induced</i>, whilst Mara is in the process of <b>being</b> induced—as though this is all a test. <br><br>Essentially <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/227463.A_Clockwork_Orange" title="A Clockwork Orange by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clockwork Orange</a> deals with the ultimate question: is mankind better off being “evil”, but at their own free will, or being conditioned to be good? In Mara Dyer, the essence of this is kept, but it is twisted and played around with. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=A%20Clockwork%20Oranges" title="A Clockwork Oranges" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clockwork Oranges</a> inaugurates the notion that perhaps the chosen evil is the lesser evil, purely because there was a moral choice. Jude seems to have lost his moral choice—the thought death and destruction doesn’t keep him awake at night. Without moral choice, a human ceases to be anything but mechanical. Mara, Noah and the rest represent the “natural man”, born “unlimited, unrepressed”, and perhaps they hold the key to where Jude went wrong. If he has been artificially manifested, should he not be conditioned as good? He is a double whammy of defiance, an experiment gone wrong, for he has not moral choice, and yet has chosen evil. This perhaps may be why they are so desperate for Mara. There may be something in her <i>genes</i> that hold the key to artificially inducing superior genes, without the mechanical cost. I don’t know if I even make sense, but I must come back and think a bit deeper about this point. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>Oh hey, I didn't forget about this. I've been thinking in the AMs lately and I've deduced something fishy about Daniel Dyer. So have this edit. </b>
</blockquote> <br><br>Now I understand that I may be hitting a sore spot for many here. I tread lightly when it comes to sibling accusations. But after relentless badgering from a friend, it seems that I can’t deny it any longer. There is something <i>off</i> about our dear <b>Daniel Dyer.</b><br><br>Hints. Clues. Details. That’s what my life has been surrounded by. My mind reads a sentence and it catches the most minuscule insignificant detail, because that’s just how it works. Sometimes I think<i> “maybe if I hoard this away, it’ll come in hand”</i> (in reality this hardly plays out, in the fictitious world, it’s a skill that comes in handy). <br><br>The position Daniel plays in all of this? I think he’s her <i>watcher</i>. It’d make him feel significant. Life has always revolved around Mara (at least in the recent years) and for a child with the brilliance of Daniel’s capacity, that can hurt. <b>A lot</b>. Jealousy brews terrible things. It drives loved ones against one another. It causes betrayal. It fractures worlds. <br><br>Daniel’s jealous of Mara. Read between the lines. What seems to be a sibling comforting the other could be interpreted completely differently. Case in point: <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“I wish Mara knew that I’m jealous of her.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote> <br><br>While we’re on that topic, Daniel is quite the expert when it comes to lying. I would find y’all the excerpt but this extra edit is coming at a time when procrastination is highly attractive, but not attractive enough to spend an hour looking for a needle in a haystack. But have this instead: <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“...but as Daniel entered the room he expertly masked his concern.”<br><br>“I said, unable to help my smile. Daniel flashed one of his own—a real one, this time.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br><br>So he’s a master at emotions. What differentiates the real Daniel from his facade? Which one is the facade? <br><br>Daniel also comes to have ownership of two things: <i>New Theories in Genetics</i> and Mara’s crazy <b>journal</b>. Despite being mysteriously elusive, Mara’s journal ends up in Daniel’s room of all places, jacked up with evidentiary support for her supposed hallucinatory state. But why Daniel’s room? <br><br>This question is left unanswered because we jump from one shock finding to another. Before we can process the journal, it turns out Daniel has <i>New Theories of Genetics</i> in his possession as well? Why? It seems a tad odd to store a book in your closet of all places. Daniel explains that he wants to separate what’s his from what’s hers but who stores things in their closet (apart from clothing) unless they don’t want it to be found (I’m so sorry if you do. No judgement here, guys.) <br><br>And then there’s the matter of 1984. Call me paranoid. Whatever. But since Orwell made a mark on me early on in my teenage years, I can’t help but shake the image, the metaphor of <b>Big Brother</b>. <b>Big Brother is always watching</b>. What I’ve deduced is that Mara has a watcher. She’s being taped inside her own house. Sure, Jude could have been the one who installed it (hell I can’t remember that scene anymore, forgive me if it’s been explained), but who else has better access than the occupants of the house. Is it a coincidence that Daniel’s keys are the ones that go missing? <br><br><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>“Daniel had kept a big-brotherly eye on me ever since”</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><br><br>Sure, that could mean that he’s a fantastic brother and I’ve just slandered him (sorry), but who knows? Big Brother, to me, has been tainted. Could he be watching her? Detailing her every move? Who knows, kids. I’ve been thinking lately (and also sleep deprived). The mind does funny things. We’ll find out, soon enough. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6857647523176510277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText4561770581946917309" style="display:none"><b>*4.25 ENTRANCED STARS*</b><br /><br /><i>
  <b>“This was the boy I loved. A little bit messy. A little bit ruined. A beautiful disaster. Just like me.” </b>
</i><br /><br />AGAIN WITH THAT CLIFFHANGER ENDING!! I <u>
  <b>CANNOT</b>
</u> HANDLE IT.<br />If I don’t manage to get my hands on the last book soon, it’s gonna be <b>my</b> ass in a mental hospital and not Mara’s!<br /><br />I really easily tend to forget what happened in books I’ve read a long time ago.<br />It’s been a year and a half since I’ve read the first book in this series, and this story sucked me back in as if no time had passed at all! That’s how <i>unforgettable</i> and <i>different</i> these books are compared to anything I’ve ever read before!</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cZUBtfGObXYamJMAY+SGeBxwtGJe6CsaodMReZOobEhLs2hnStCkHr2zJ2GUEYynyi9TqpBlpX36rVfIXuue0g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('u751jGr/Zv4q+wTVK7zpFYlE8KGbP42TZfjqPvxe7GiBmBxe0aGvlo3QsLTcSePKXxsXaVWyA/Q+hqyPMR0e8g==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4126827.Michelle_Hodkin">About Michelle Hodkin</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4126827.Michelle_Hodkin"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1594978408p3/4126827.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4126827.Michelle_Hodkin">Michelle Hodkin
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
11,958
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1196505011098472740">Michelle Hodkin is the author of the Mara Dyer Trilogy, which was a New York Times, USA Today, and Publishers Weekly bestselling series. The trilogy, which includes The Unbecoming of Mara Dyer, The Evolution of Mara Dyer, and The Retribution of Mara Dyer, was described as “haunting and dreamlike” by Cassandra Clare and “darkly funny, deliciously creepy, and genuinely thoughtful” by Veronica Roth. </span>
  <span id="freeText1196505011098472740" style="display:none">Michelle Hodkin is the author of the Mara Dyer Trilogy, which was a New York Times, USA Today, and Publishers Weekly bestselling series. The trilogy, which includes The Unbecoming of Mara Dyer, The Evolution of Mara Dyer, and The Retribution of Mara Dyer, was described as “haunting and dreamlike” by Cassandra Clare and “darkly funny, deliciously creepy, and genuinely thoughtful” by Veronica Roth. Lev Grossman has called Hodkin “One of the greatest talents in Young Adult fiction.” The novels were praised by Romantic Times, MTV’s Hollywood Crush, and the Los Angeles Times, and books from the series appeared on several state reading lists. Additionally, The Retribution of Mara Dyer was selected as one of TIME.com’s Top 10 YA Books of 2014. Hodkin grew up in Florida, went to college in New York, and studied law in Michigan, before finally settling in Brooklyn last year. <br /><br />You can visit her online at:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.michellehodkin.com/">http://www.michellehodkin.com/</a><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://twitter.com/MichelleHodkin">http://twitter.com/MichelleHodkin</a> <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://pinterest.com/michellehodkin/">http://pinterest.com/michellehodkin/</a><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://michellehodkin.tumblr.com/">http://michellehodkin.tumblr.com/</a> </span>
  <a data-text-id="1196505011098472740" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
